Refutation of Two Eternals and an Eternal Foolish Being
Nasafi refutes the possibility of a second eternal and an eternal foolish being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theology rejects the possibility of a second eternal being and an eternal foolish being, based on the conditions of eternity and the argument of mutual hindrance.

Core Idea
Eternity demands perfection; a deficient eternal is a contradiction.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi begins his refutation of the Magian claim that there are two eternal principles—one good and one evil. He argues that the very concept of eternity is incompatible with deficiency, and that positing a second eternal leads to absurdities. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the refutation
The Magians (Zoroastrians) believed in two eternal principles: Yazdān (good) and Ahriman (evil). Nasafi is responding to their doubt by showing the impossibility of a second eternal being.
This refutation is part of a larger discussion on divine attributes and sectarian debates.
2
Examine the first argument: no second eternal
Nasafi states that the proofs already established necessitate the invalidity of another eternal alongside Allah. Therefore, the claim of two eternals is false.
The earlier proof likely refers to arguments based on mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) or the impossibility of two necessary existents.
3
Analyze the second argument: eternal foolish being is impossible
Nasafi argues that perfection is a condition of eternity. A deficient eternal being (like a foolish one) would entail a contradiction because deficiency necessitates non-existence or the need for another.
Foolishness implies lack of wisdom, which is a deficiency. An eternal being cannot be deficient.
4
Consider the alternative view and its implication
Even if one claims Ahriman is originated (not eternal), he cannot have the power to create because that would imply mutual hindrance between two powerful beings, which has already been refuted.
This shows that all possibilities lead to the corruption of the Magian position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة كلامهم: إلا أنا نقول: إن ما أقمنا من الدلالة موجب بطلان أن يكون مع الله تعالى قديم آخر، فبطل القول بقدم اثنين ولأن القول بقدم السفيه ناقص محال، إذ من شرط القدم الكمال لأن ذاتا ما لا يوجب نقصه لا في ذاته ولا في صفاته، إذ ذلك اقتضاء عدمه، وذات ما لا يقتضي عدمه، إذ لو اقتضاه لما تصور وجوده فإذا كان نقص تمكن في ذات ما فذلك بإثبات غيره، ليعرف بنقصان هذا كمال مثبته، ولو كان أهر من محدثا على قول القائلين: إنه محدث، فلا ينبغي أن يكون له قدرة الإيجاد ( لما فيه ) من إثبات التمانع، وقد بينا فساد ذلك.
Discussion of their words: But we say: Indeed, what we have established as proof necessitates the invalidity of there being another eternal alongside Allah, may He be exalted. So the statement of two eternals is invalidated. And because the statement of an eternal foolish being is deficient and impossible, since perfection is a condition of eternity, because any essence that does not necessitate its deficiency in itself or in its attributes, since that would entail its non-existence, and any essence that does not necessitate its non-existence, since if it necessitated it, its existence would not be conceivable. So when deficiency is inherent in some essence, that is by the positing of another, so that by the deficiency of this one, the perfection of the one positing it is known. And if Ahriman is originated, according to those who say he is originated, then he should not have the power of creation, because of what it entails of proving mutual hindrance (al-tamānuʿ), and we have already shown the corruption of that.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the refutation (munāqashah) of the Magian doubt. The author states that earlier proofs have already established the impossibility of another eternal being alongside Allah. Therefore, the claim of two eternals is false. Then he argues that the idea of an eternal foolish being is impossible because eternity requires perfection. A deficient eternal being would entail a contradiction. Furthermore, even if one claims that Ahriman is originated, he cannot have the power to create because that would imply mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) between two powerful beings, which is already refuted.

Key Terms
قديم
سفيه
نقص
كمال
تمانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that an eternal being can be deficient, e.g., a foolish eternal being is possible.
Correction: Kalam asserts that eternity entails absolute perfection; any deficiency in an eternal being would imply its non-existence or dependence on another, which is contradictory to the concept of eternity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that an eternal foolish being is impossible?
A) Because foolishness is a deficiency and eternity requires perfection
B) Because a foolish being cannot exist at all
C) Because the Magians themselves deny it
D) Because only one eternal being can exist

Key Takeaway
Eternity entails absolute perfection, so any deficiency invalidates eternity, and the existence of two eternals is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's perfection is necessary for His eternity, what does that mean for your own understanding of His attributes? In what ways do you still attribute human-like deficiencies to Allah in your heart, and what one step can you take today to purify your tawhid?
?
The argument shows that any deficiency in a supposed eternal being points to a perfect other. How does this challenge you to see every imperfection in creation as a sign pointing to the Perfect Creator? Commit to practicing this reflection daily for the next week.
?
Consider the error of the Magians who posited a foolish eternal being. Are there any 'foolish' beliefs or practices you have held onto that contradict Allah's perfection? What would it cost you to let them go, and are you willing to pay that price for the sake of true tawhid?

Demanding Proof for Divine Wisdom
Nasafi rejects the objection and demands its basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi initially refutes the objection by demanding the basis of the claim—whether it is from revelation or reason.

Core Idea
Claims about God require proof; unproven objections are invalid.

The objectors argue that a wise God cannot create evil because that would be foolish. Imam Nasafi begins his refutation by rejecting this claim outright and then interrogating the grounds on which it is made. He asks a fundamental question: what proof do you have—scriptural or rational? This lesson covers the opening move of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then consult the translation. Notice the direct tone: 'it is rejected' (ممنوع).
2
Identify the two types of proof
Look for the contrast between revealed proof (دليل سمعي) and rational proof (دليل عقلي). The author demands that the objectors specify which kind they rely on.
This sets up the structure of the refutation.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why is it important to demand proof? Because if the objection is based on revelation, its authority must be established; if on reason, the argument must be presented. Nasafi places the burden of proof on the objectors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا من استحالة إيجاد الحكيم الشرور والقبايح، لكون ذلك كله سفها فهو ممنوع، فيقال لهم: لم قلتم ذلك؟ ومن أين قلتم؟ أبدليل سمعي قلتم أم بدليل عقلي؟ فإن ادعوا الدليل السمعي، طلب منهم الدليل على كون قول من أسندوا القول إليه حجة، وإن ادعوا الدليل العقلي طولبوا بإيراده، ثم نقول لهم: أعرفتم بعقولكم أن إيجاد هذه الأشياء سفه أم حكمتم بكونه سفها لجهلكم بوجه الحكمة في إيجاد هذه الأشياء؟ فإن زعموا أنهم عرفوا بعقولهم كونه سفها، قيل لهم: أببديهة العقل عرفتم ذلك أم بالاستدلال؟ فإن قالوا عرفنا بالبديهة ظهرت مكابرتهم، لأن أرباب العقول السليمة لا يختلفون معرفة البديهيات ونحن وجميع من ساعدنا في وحدانية الصانع لا نعرف هذا، فإن قالوا: إنكم تعرفون ذلك وتجحدون، قيل لهم وبم تنفصلون ممن قال لكم: إنكم تعرفون فساد قولكم وصحة قول خصومكم بالبديهة، غير أنكم تعاندون؟ فإن قالوا: عرفنا ذلك بالاستدلال، طولبوا بإقامة الدليل، وإن قالوا: في الشاهد كل من أوجد شرا فهو شرير ومن أوجد قبيحا فهو سفيه، قلنا: ولم إذا كان في الشاهد هكذا، يكون في الغائب كذلك؟ وبأي معنى تجمعون بين الشاهد والغائب؟ على أن هذا ممنوع أن في الشاهد يكون على ما يزعمون، فإن في الشاهد من كان فعله شرا قبيحا فهو شرير سفيه، فأما من كان مفعوله كذلك فلا، ثم إنه شرير سفيه، وفي المتنازع فيه ما هو شر وقبيح مفعول الله تعالى لا فعله وإيجاده ثم في الشاهد إنما يكون الفعل سفها قبيحا شرا إذا لم يكن له عاقبة حميدة، فأما ما له عاقبة حميدة فليس شرا ولا سفها ولا قبيحا، فلم قلتم إن إيجاد الله تعالى هذه الأشياء ليست له عاقبة حميدة؟ فإن قالوا: حكمنا بكون هذه الأشياء سفها، لأنا لا نعرف فيه حكمة، ولم نقف على جهة الحكمة، قيل لهم: أكل ما لا تعرفون لا يكون؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، ظهرت مكابرتهم، وبان عنادهم وإن قالوا: قد يكون ما لا نعرف قيل لهم ما أنكرتم أن الله تعالى في إيجاد ذلك حكمة لا تبلغها أفهامكم لأنكم تعجزون عن الوقوف على جميع الحكم البشرية، فكيف تدعون الوقوف على جميع الحكم الربوبية؟
And what they said about the impossibility of the Wise One creating evils and ugly things, because all of that is foolishness, is rejected. So it is said to them: Why did you say that? And from where did you say it? Did you say it by a revealed proof or by a rational proof? If they claim the revealed proof, the proof that the one to whom they attribute the saying is authoritative is demanded from them. And if they claim the rational proof, they are required to bring it. Then we say to them: Do you know with your intellects that creating these things is foolishness, or did you judge it to be foolishness out of ignorance of the aspect of wisdom in creating these things? If they claim that they know with their intellects that it is foolishness, it is said to them: Did you know that by direct intuition of the intellect or by deduction? If they say: We knew by direct intuition, their obstinacy becomes apparent, because the people of sound intellects do not differ in knowledge of intuitive matters, and we and all who agree with us on the oneness of the Creator do not know this. If they say: You know that but you deny it, it is said to them: How do you distinguish yourselves from one who says to you: You know by direct intuition the corruption of your statement and the soundness of your opponents' statement, but you are being obstinate? If they say: We knew that by deduction, they are required to present the proof. And if they say: In the witnessed world, whoever creates evil is evil, and whoever creates ugliness is foolish, we say: Why, if it is like that in the witnessed world, should it be like that in the unseen? And in what sense do you analogize between the witnessed and the unseen? Moreover, this is rejected that in the witnessed world it is as they claim. For in the witnessed world, one whose act is evil and ugly is evil and foolish. But as for one whose effect (maf'ul) is like that, no. Then he is evil and foolish. And in the disputed matter, what is evil and ugly is the effect of God, not His act and bringing into existence. Then in the witnessed world, an act is only foolish, ugly, and evil if it has no praiseworthy outcome. As for what has a praiseworthy outcome, it is not evil, nor foolish, nor ugly. So why did you say that God's creating these things has no praiseworthy outcome? If they say: We judged these things to be foolish because we do not know any wisdom in them and have not grasped the aspect of wisdom, it is said to them: Is everything you do not know non-existent? If they say: Yes, their obstinacy appears and their stubbornness becomes clear. And if they say: It may be that we do not know, it is said to them: What prevents you from acknowledging that God in creating that has a wisdom that your intellects cannot reach, because you are incapable of grasping all human wisdoms, so how do you claim to grasp all divine wisdoms?
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that a wise God cannot create evil and ugly things, because doing so would be foolish. The author first rejects the objection as invalid. Then he interrogates the objectors: by what proof do they claim this—revelation or reason? If revelation, they must prove its authority; if reason, they must present the argument. He then asks whether they know by direct intuition (badiha) or by deduction (istidlal) that creating these things is foolish. If by intuition, he points out that people of sound intellect do not differ on intuitive matters, and many (including the author) do not intuitively know that. If the objectors claim that we do know but deny it, the author retorts that the same could be said in reverse. If by deduction, they must produce the proof. If they analogize to the witnessed world—that whoever creates evil is evil—the author challenges the analogy: why assume the unseen is like the witnessed? Moreover, even in the witnessed world, the one whose effect (maf'ul) is evil is not necessarily evil; it is the act (fi'l) that is evil. And in this case, the evil is God's effect, not His act. Furthermore, in the witnessed world, an act is only considered foolish if it has no good outcome; if it has a good outcome, it is not foolish. So the objectors must prove that God's creations have no good outcome. If they say they don't know any wisdom, the author asks: is everything you don't know non-existent? If they say yes, they are obstinate; if they admit it might exist, they cannot deny that God may have wisdom beyond human understanding. The passage ends by highlighting human inability to grasp all divine wisdoms.

Key Terms
الحكيم
الشرور
القبايح
السفه
الدليل السمعي
الدليل العقلي

Checkpoint
What two types of proof does Nasafi ask the objectors to identify?
A) Analogical and intuitive
B) Revealed and rational
C) Inductive and deductive
D) Empirical and speculative

Key Takeaway
The objection that God's creation of evil is foolish is rejected outright, and the burden is placed on the objectors to produce either a scriptural or rational proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doubt about God's wisdom in your own life, do you immediately challenge the assumption behind that doubt, or do you let it settle in your heart? What would it mean for you to demand proof from yourself as Nasafi demands from the objectors?
?
Have you ever assumed that your personal sense of what is 'wise' or 'foolish' is enough to judge God's actions? Are you willing to subject that assumption to either revealed or rational proof today?
?
The objectors in this lesson are asked, 'Why did you say that? And from where did you say it?' Think of a belief you hold about God that you have never examined. Will you take the step to question its foundation now?

Knowledge by Intuition or Deduction?
Nasafi questions the source of the objectors' knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi challenges the objectors' claim to know that creating evil is foolish, by distinguishing between intuitive and deductive knowledge.

Core Idea
Intuitive knowledge is universal; if it's not, your claim lacks foundation.

After demanding proof, Nasafi probes deeper: do the objectors actually 'know' that creating evil is foolish, or are they just ignorant of the wisdom behind it? He then examines whether their knowledge, if claimed, comes from direct intuition (badiha) or logical deduction (istidlal). This lesson focuses on this crucial epistemological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two options given to the objectors: 'Do you know with your intellects... or did you judge out of ignorance...?'
2
Trace the argument on badiha
If they claim knowledge by badiha, Nasafi retorts that intuitive matters are universally agreed upon by sound intellects, yet many do not share their intuition. This shows their claim is false.
The counter-argument about mutual accusations of obstinacy is also important.
3
Trace the argument on istidlal
If they claim deduction, they must produce an actual proof. Nasafi demands they present it. This forces them to articulate their reasoning.
4
Reflect on the strength of the refutation
By showing that neither path—badiha nor istidlal—works for the objectors, Nasafi undermines their entire epistemological basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowing something 'intuitively' is sufficient to prove a theological claim.
Correction: Nasafi shows that genuine intuitive knowledge is universal and undeniable; if others do not share it, the claim is not intuitive but subjective or based on ignorance.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the characteristic of true intuitive knowledge (badiha)?
A) It is only known by scholars.
B) It is universally agreed upon by people of sound intellect.
C) It can change over time.
D) It requires proof.

Key Takeaway
The objectors' claim to know that creating evil is foolish fails either as intuition (not universal) or as deduction (unproven).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation in life that seems pointless or unjust, do you immediately assume folly on God's part? How would this passage confront you to instead admit your ignorance and trust His wisdom?
?
Is there a belief you hold about God that you've always assumed was 'obvious'—yet you've never tested whether it truly is intuitive to all sound minds? What would it cost you to honestly examine that belief?
?
Nasafi shows that claiming deduction without proof is empty. Are there areas of your faith where you rely on vague reasoning without solid evidence? How can you move from comfortable assumptions to knowledge that can withstand scrutiny?

Divine Attributes

6 min
Analogies and Divine Wisdom
Nasafi refutes the analogy and argues for the limits of human knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the analogy from the witnessed world and argues that we cannot limit God's wisdom to our understanding.

Core Idea
What seems evil may have a wise purpose beyond human grasp.

The objectors analogize from human experience: in this world, whoever creates evil is evil. Therefore, God must be evil. Nasafi dismantles this analogy in multiple steps. He questions the validity of the analogy itself, distinguishes between act and effect, notes that evil actions can have good outcomes, and finally argues that human ignorance of wisdom does not mean wisdom is absent. This lesson covers these powerful refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the full Arabic and translation. Notice the many objections Nasafi raises in rapid succession.
2
Examine the critique of analogy
Nasafi questions why the unseen (غائب) must be like the witnessed (شاهد). He also points out that even in the witnessed world, the one whose effect is evil is not necessarily evil—the act is evil, not the effect.
The distinction between fi'l (act) and maf'ul (effect) is crucial.
3
Consider the condition of outcome
Nasafi adds that an act is only considered foolish if it has no good outcome. Since we don't know the ultimate outcomes of God's actions, we cannot call them foolish.
4
Reflect on the argument from ignorance
Finally, if the objectors say they see no wisdom, Nasafi points out that lack of knowledge does not imply non-existence. Human minds cannot grasp all divine wisdoms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا من استحالة إيجاد الحكيم الشرور والقبايح، لكون ذلك كله سفها فهو ممنوع، فيقال لهم: لم قلتم ذلك؟ ومن أين قلتم؟ أبدليل سمعي قلتم أم بدليل عقلي؟ فإن ادعوا الدليل السمعي، طلب منهم الدليل على كون قول من أسندوا القول إليه حجة، وإن ادعوا الدليل العقلي طولبوا بإيراده، ثم نقول لهم: أعرفتم بعقولكم أن إيجاد هذه الأشياء سفه أم حكمتم بكونه سفها لجهلكم بوجه الحكمة في إيجاد هذه الأشياء؟ فإن زعموا أنهم عرفوا بعقولهم كونه سفها، قيل لهم: أببديهة العقل عرفتم ذلك أم بالاستدلال؟ فإن قالوا عرفنا بالبديهة ظهرت مكابرتهم، لأن أرباب العقول السليمة لا يختلفون معرفة البديهيات ونحن وجميع من ساعدنا في وحدانية الصانع لا نعرف هذا، فإن قالوا: إنكم تعرفون ذلك وتجحدون، قيل لهم وبم تنفصلون ممن قال لكم: إنكم تعرفون فساد قولكم وصحة قول خصومكم بالبديهة، غير أنكم تعاندون؟ فإن قالوا: عرفنا ذلك بالاستدلال، طولبوا بإقامة الدليل، وإن قالوا: في الشاهد كل من أوجد شرا فهو شرير ومن أوجد قبيحا فهو سفيه، قلنا: ولم إذا كان في الشاهد هكذا، يكون في الغائب كذلك؟ وبأي معنى تجمعون بين الشاهد والغائب؟ على أن هذا ممنوع أن في الشاهد يكون على ما يزعمون، فإن في الشاهد من كان فعله شرا قبيحا فهو شرير سفيه، فأما من كان مفعوله كذلك فلا، ثم إنه شرير سفيه، وفي المتنازع فيه ما هو شر وقبيح مفعول الله تعالى لا فعله وإيجاده ثم في الشاهد إنما يكون الفعل سفها قبيحا شرا إذا لم يكن له عاقبة حميدة، فأما ما له عاقبة حميدة فليس شرا ولا سفها ولا قبيحا، فلم قلتم إن إيجاد الله تعالى هذه الأشياء ليست له عاقبة حميدة؟ فإن قالوا: حكمنا بكون هذه الأشياء سفها، لأنا لا نعرف فيه حكمة، ولم نقف على جهة الحكمة، قيل لهم: أكل ما لا تعرفون لا يكون؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، ظهرت مكابرتهم، وبان عنادهم وإن قالوا: قد يكون ما لا نعرف قيل لهم ما أنكرتم أن الله تعالى في إيجاد ذلك حكمة لا تبلغها أفهامكم لأنكم تعجزون عن الوقوف على جميع الحكم البشرية، فكيف تدعون الوقوف على جميع الحكم الربوبية؟
And what they said about the impossibility of the Wise One creating evils and ugly things, because all of that is foolishness, is rejected. So it is said to them: Why did you say that? And from where did you say it? Did you say it by a revealed proof or by a rational proof? If they claim the revealed proof, the proof that the one to whom they attribute the saying is authoritative is demanded from them. And if they claim the rational proof, they are required to bring it. Then we say to them: Do you know with your intellects that creating these things is foolishness, or did you judge it to be foolishness out of ignorance of the aspect of wisdom in creating these things? If they claim that they know with their intellects that it is foolishness, it is said to them: Did you know that by direct intuition of the intellect or by deduction? If they say: We knew by direct intuition, their obstinacy becomes apparent, because the people of sound intellects do not differ in knowledge of intuitive matters, and we and all who agree with us on the oneness of the Creator do not know this. If they say: You know that but you deny it, it is said to them: How do you distinguish yourselves from one who says to you: You know by direct intuition the corruption of your statement and the soundness of your opponents' statement, but you are being obstinate? If they say: We knew that by deduction, they are required to present the proof. And if they say: In the witnessed world, whoever creates evil is evil, and whoever creates ugliness is foolish, we say: Why, if it is like that in the witnessed world, should it be like that in the unseen? And in what sense do you analogize between the witnessed and the unseen? Moreover, this is rejected that in the witnessed world it is as they claim. For in the witnessed world, one whose act is evil and ugly is evil and foolish. But as for one whose effect (maf'ul) is like that, no. Then he is evil and foolish. And in the disputed matter, what is evil and ugly is the effect of God, not His act and bringing into existence. Then in the witnessed world, an act is only foolish, ugly, and evil if it has no praiseworthy outcome. As for what has a praiseworthy outcome, it is not evil, nor foolish, nor ugly. So why did you say that God's creating these things has no praiseworthy outcome? If they say: We judged these things to be foolish because we do not know any wisdom in them and have not grasped the aspect of wisdom, it is said to them: Is everything you do not know non-existent? If they say: Yes, their obstinacy appears and their stubbornness becomes clear. And if they say: It may be that we do not know, it is said to them: What prevents you from acknowledging that God in creating that has a wisdom that your intellects cannot reach, because you are incapable of grasping all human wisdoms, so how do you claim to grasp all divine wisdoms?
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that a wise God cannot create evil and ugly things, because doing so would be foolish. The author first rejects the objection as invalid. Then he interrogates the objectors: by what proof do they claim this—revelation or reason? If revelation, they must prove its authority; if reason, they must present the argument. He then asks whether they know by direct intuition (badiha) or by deduction (istidlal) that creating these things is foolish. If by intuition, he points out that people of sound intellect do not differ on intuitive matters, and many (including the author) do not intuitively know that. If the objectors claim that we do know but deny it, the author retorts that the same could be said in reverse. If by deduction, they must produce the proof. If they analogize to the witnessed world—that whoever creates evil is evil—the author challenges the analogy: why assume the unseen is like the witnessed? Moreover, even in the witnessed world, the one whose effect (maf'ul) is evil is not necessarily evil; it is the act (fi'l) that is evil. And in this case, the evil is God's effect, not His act. Furthermore, in the witnessed world, an act is only considered foolish if it has no good outcome; if it has a good outcome, it is not foolish. So the objectors must prove that God's creations have no good outcome. If they say they don't know any wisdom, the author asks: is everything you don't know non-existent? If they say yes, they are obstinate; if they admit it might exist, they cannot deny that God may have wisdom beyond human understanding. The passage ends by highlighting human inability to grasp all divine wisdoms.

الغائب
المفعول
الفعل
العاقبة الحميدة
الحكمة الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that because we see evil in the world, God must be evil or foolish.
Correction: Nasafi argues that we cannot analogize from human actions to divine actions because God's effects are not His acts in the same sense, and we are ignorant of the ultimate outcomes and wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the key distinction that undermines the analogy from the witnessed world?
A) The witnessed world is more complex than the unseen.
B) In the witnessed world, evil is always punished.
C) The one whose effect is evil is not necessarily evil; only the one whose act is evil is evil.
D) God is not the creator of evil.

Key Takeaway
The analogy from human experience fails because of key distinctions between act and effect, and because we cannot assume lack of wisdom where we lack knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter suffering or evil in your life, do you instinctively assume it is meaningless? How does this lesson challenge you to trust in divine wisdom even when you cannot see the purpose?
?
Are you willing to admit that your intellect is limited and that God's wisdom may far exceed your understanding? How would your attitude toward trials change if you truly believed that?
?
You judge many things as 'bad' or 'unfair' based on your immediate perception. But this lesson says you cannot grasp all human wisdoms, let alone divine ones. How will you now respond when your own reasoning questions God's actions?

The Wisdom of Diversity in Creation
Nasafi argues that diversity proves God's perfect power and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the world's diversity—its varying parts and opposite effects—demonstrates the perfection of God's power and will.

Core Idea
Diversity in creation is a sign of God's absolute power and free will.

Nasafi presents the second argument for divine wisdom: why did God create a world with such diversity, including both beneficial and harmful things? He argues that this variety itself is evidence of God's perfect power and will, unlike a being that acts in only one fixed way. This passage unpacks that reasoning and shows how even opposites point to a single wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the example
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note how the author contrasts a being that acts in one way (like fire always heating) with God's ability to create opposites.
Focus on the analogy of fire and snow.
2
Understand the logical inference
The author argues that if God only produced one kind of effect, it would imply He is compelled like natural forces. But His creation of opposites proves His perfect power and free will.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the creation of all things—both beneficial and harmful—is wisdom, because they together testify to the Creator's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنافع ليصح الوعد والإيعاد والترغيب والترهيب والله الموفق. والثاني: أنه خلق العالم مختلف الأجزاء متفاوت الأبعاض، متباين الأفعال والآثار من نافع وضار ومستحسن ومستقبح حار وبارد وخشن ولين وغير ذلك، ليستدل بذلك على كمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وسلطانه، إذ من فعله على جهة واحدة فهو كالمطبوع المسخر على ذلك كالنار على التسخين والثلج على التبريد، فيكون في خلقه المختلفات إقامة دلالة كمال القدرة ونفاذ المشيئة وهذه عاقبة حميدة، فكان خلق الكل حكمة وكذا لما اجتمعت هذه الأجسام المختلفة على الدلالة على أن لها صانعا حكيما لا يسفه، عالما لا يجهل قادرا لا يعجز كانت دليل كمال القدرة وتمام التدبير ونفاذ المشيئة، حيث جعل المتضادات والمختلفات بالجوهر متجانسا في حق الشهادة على ربوبيته وحكمته وكمال قدرته.
والثالث: أنه خلق الأجسام الضارة والأوجاع والآلام ليذل بها الجبابرة والملوك، ليعلموا بذلك ضعفهم، فلا يفتروا بكثرة العدة والعتاد، واجتماع الجنود والعساكر، والأتباع والحواشي، فيصير ذلك مبعثة لهم على المسارعة إلى طاعته، ومزجرة عن الانهماك في معاصيه وداعيا إلى إفاضة العدل فيمن تحت أيديهم من الرعايا، وصادًا لهم عن الجور والظلم على من في ممالكهم من أصناف البرايا.
والرابع: أنه خلق هذه الأشياء الضارة المستقذرة ليعرف من رآها أنه تعالى منشئ العالم غني أن ينتفع بشيء أو يحتاج إلى شيء إذ من ذلك وصفه لا يخرج فعله عما ينتفع به، وتندفع به حاجته فكان في تخليق هذه الأشياء إظهار غناه وتعاليه عن الحاجة، ووراء هذه المعاني التي بيناها معان كثيرة ذكرها الشيخ الإمام أبو منصور - رحمه الله - في مسائل التعديل والتجوير من كتاب التوحيد أعرضنا عن ذكرها، واكتفينا بهذا القدر لئلا يؤدي إلى التطويل مع حصول البغته والمقصود بهذا القدر مع أنا غير محتاجين إلى بيان هذه الأشياء فإنا إذا أقمنا الدلالة على وحدانية الصانع، وامتناع القول بالصانعين وعلى كون العالم بجميع أجزائه محدثاً ودلالته على منشئه، وعلى كون منشئه حكيما لا يسفه ولا يجهل، ثبت كون تخليق هذه الأشياء حكمة بضرورة هذه المقدمات، فبقي بعد ذلك جهل الخصوم بوجه الحكمة، وذا لا يوجب خروجه عن الحكمة على ما قررنا والله الوفق.
And the beneficial [creation is] to validate promise and threat, and encouragement and deterrence, and God is the grantor of success. The second: that He created the world with different parts, varying in limbs, diverse in actions and effects, from beneficial and harmful, good and bad, hot and cold, rough and soft, and other than that, so that by that it may be inferred the perfection of His power, the execution of His will, and His sovereignty; for whoever does a thing in one manner is like the imprinted subjugated to that, like fire for heating and snow for cooling. So in His creating differences there is the establishment of evidence of perfection of power and execution of will, and this is a praiseworthy outcome. So the creation of all is wisdom. Likewise, when these different bodies assembled upon indicating that they have a wise maker who does not behave foolishly, a knowing one who is not ignorant, a powerful one who is not incapable, they are an evidence of perfection of power, completeness of management, and execution of will, since He made the opposites and differences, in respect of substance, homogeneous in the right of testimony to His lordship, His wisdom, and the perfection of His power. The third: that He created harmful bodies, pains, and ailments to humble by them the tyrants and kings, so that they may know by that their weakness, and not become insolent due to abundance of equipment and preparedness, and the gathering of armies and soldiers, followers and retinues. That becomes a motive for them to hasten to His obedience, a deterrent from plunging into His disobedience, an invitation to dispensing justice to those under their hands of subjects, and a prevention from injustice and oppression against those in their kingdoms from kinds of creatures. The fourth: that He created these harmful, disgusting things so that whoever sees them may know that He, exalted, is the originator of the world, independent, not benefiting from anything nor needing anything; for if that were not His description, His action would not go beyond what benefits and fulfills need. So in the creation of these things there is manifestation of His independence and His exaltation above need. Beyond these meanings that we have explained, there are many meanings that the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur - may God have mercy on him - mentioned in the chapters of al-Ta'dil wa al-Tajwir from the book al-Tawhid; we have refrained from mentioning them and sufficed with this amount, lest it lead to prolongation while the goal is achieved. The purpose with this amount, while we are not in need of explaining these things, for when we establish evidence of the oneness of the Maker, the impossibility of claiming two makers, that the world in all its parts is originated, its indication of its Originator, and that its Originator is wise, not foolish and not ignorant, the wisdom of creating these things is established necessarily from these premises. Then after that, the ignorance of the opponents of the aspect of wisdom remains, and that does not necessitate its departure from wisdom, as we have established. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (the second, third, and fourth) for the wisdom (ḥikma) in creation, following an initial point (not included here) about beneficial things validating divine promises and threats. The second argument states that God created the world with diverse parts, actions, and effects—both beneficial and harmful, good and bad, etc.—so that this diversity demonstrates the perfection of His power (qudra), the execution of His will (mashīʼa), and His sovereignty. The author notes that if a being acts in only one way, it is like a compelled force (e.g., fire always heats), but God's ability to produce opposites proves His absolute power and free will. The third argument states that God created harmful bodies, pains, and ailments to humble tyrants and kings, reminding them of their weakness so they do not become arrogant. This serves as a motive for obedience, a deterrent from sin, and an encouragement to justice. The fourth argument states that God created disgusting and harmful things to show that He is independent (ghanī) and has no needs, for a being that only creates beneficial things would appear dependent. Therefore, creating harmful things manifests His transcendence above need. The passage then concludes that these arguments are sufficient; the author refrains from listing all such purposes mentioned by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He emphasizes that once the core doctrines (oneness of God, creation, divine wisdom) are established, the wisdom of all creations follows necessarily. The opponents' ignorance of the specific wisdom does not negate its existence.

حكمة
قدرة
مشيئة
مختلفات
متفاوت
متباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Diversity and opposites in creation suggest randomness or multiple creators.
Correction: Nasafi argues that diversity actually proves one all-powerful Creator, as it demonstrates His freedom and perfection—unlike a compelled being that only produces one effect.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does the diversity of creation—like fire and snow—primarily demonstrate?
A) That nature is self-sufficient
B) The perfection of God's power and will
C) That good and evil are equal
D) The randomness of creation

Key Takeaway
The diversity of creation—beneficial and harmful, hot and cold—points to the perfection of one Creator's power and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face something 'harmful' or 'bad' in your life, do you truly believe it is from the same God who gives 'beneficial' and 'good'? Or do you secretly think it is from chance or something else? Examine your heart: have you ever complained against God when hardship came, forgetting that both ease and difficulty are signs of His perfect will? Commit today to see every opposite as testifying to the same Lord, and thank Him for both.
?
The passage says that if God only acted one way, He would be like fire—compelled. Do you treat God as though He is bound by your expectations, demanding He always give you comfort and success? How can you break free from this and worship the God who freely creates all things, even what you dislike? Write one concrete way you will accept a current hardship as part of His wise plan.
?
All diverse bodies are 'homogeneous in testimony' to God's lordship. Look at your own life: can you see how different circumstances—joy and sorrow, health and sickness—all equally point to one Creator? What is one area where you focus on the difference rather than the unified testimony? Reflect and repent, and determine to see His hand in every situation.

The Wisdom of Harmful Things: Humbling the Powerful
Nasafi explains that harmful creation humbles tyrants and promotes justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the purpose behind creating harmful bodies, pains, and ailments—to humble tyrants and kings and lead them to justice.

Core Idea
Pains and ailments humble the powerful, turning them toward obedience and justice.

The third argument in Nasafi's series addresses a common question: why does God allow harmful and painful things? Rather than being meaningless, these things serve a profound moral purpose. They remind the powerful of their weakness, deter arrogance, and promote justice among rulers. This passage shows how even suffering has wisdom in the divine plan.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main beneficiaries
Note that the passage specifically mentions 'tyrants and kings' as the target of this wisdom. Pains and ailments are created to humble them.
2
Trace the chain of effects
Follow the logical chain: humility → knowledge of weakness → prevention of arrogance → motivation for obedience → deterrence from sin → encouragement to justice → prevention of oppression.
This is a comprehensive moral effect.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this answers objections that harmful creation is pointless. The purpose is ethical reform of society through the humbling of rulers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنافع ليصح الوعد والإيعاد والترغيب والترهيب والله الموفق. والثاني: أنه خلق العالم مختلف الأجزاء متفاوت الأبعاض، متباين الأفعال والآثار من نافع وضار ومستحسن ومستقبح حار وبارد وخشن ولين وغير ذلك، ليستدل بذلك على كمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وسلطانه، إذ من فعله على جهة واحدة فهو كالمطبوع المسخر على ذلك كالنار على التسخين والثلج على التبريد، فيكون في خلقه المختلفات إقامة دلالة كمال القدرة ونفاذ المشيئة وهذه عاقبة حميدة، فكان خلق الكل حكمة وكذا لما اجتمعت هذه الأجسام المختلفة على الدلالة على أن لها صانعا حكيما لا يسفه، عالما لا يجهل قادرا لا يعجز كانت دليل كمال القدرة وتمام التدبير ونفاذ المشيئة، حيث جعل المتضادات والمختلفات بالجوهر متجانسا في حق الشهادة على ربوبيته وحكمته وكمال قدرته.
والثالث: أنه خلق الأجسام الضارة والأوجاع والآلام ليذل بها الجبابرة والملوك، ليعلموا بذلك ضعفهم، فلا يفتروا بكثرة العدة والعتاد، واجتماع الجنود والعساكر، والأتباع والحواشي، فيصير ذلك مبعثة لهم على المسارعة إلى طاعته، ومزجرة عن الانهماك في معاصيه وداعيا إلى إفاضة العدل فيمن تحت أيديهم من الرعايا، وصادًا لهم عن الجور والظلم على من في ممالكهم من أصناف البرايا.
والرابع: أنه خلق هذه الأشياء الضارة المستقذرة ليعرف من رآها أنه تعالى منشئ العالم غني أن ينتفع بشيء أو يحتاج إلى شيء إذ من ذلك وصفه لا يخرج فعله عما ينتفع به، وتندفع به حاجته فكان في تخليق هذه الأشياء إظهار غناه وتعاليه عن الحاجة، ووراء هذه المعاني التي بيناها معان كثيرة ذكرها الشيخ الإمام أبو منصور - رحمه الله - في مسائل التعديل والتجوير من كتاب التوحيد أعرضنا عن ذكرها، واكتفينا بهذا القدر لئلا يؤدي إلى التطويل مع حصول البغته والمقصود بهذا القدر مع أنا غير محتاجين إلى بيان هذه الأشياء فإنا إذا أقمنا الدلالة على وحدانية الصانع، وامتناع القول بالصانعين وعلى كون العالم بجميع أجزائه محدثاً ودلالته على منشئه، وعلى كون منشئه حكيما لا يسفه ولا يجهل، ثبت كون تخليق هذه الأشياء حكمة بضرورة هذه المقدمات، فبقي بعد ذلك جهل الخصوم بوجه الحكمة، وذا لا يوجب خروجه عن الحكمة على ما قررنا والله الوفق.
And the beneficial [creation is] to validate promise and threat, and encouragement and deterrence, and God is the grantor of success. The second: that He created the world with different parts, varying in limbs, diverse in actions and effects, from beneficial and harmful, good and bad, hot and cold, rough and soft, and other than that, so that by that it may be inferred the perfection of His power, the execution of His will, and His sovereignty; for whoever does a thing in one manner is like the imprinted subjugated to that, like fire for heating and snow for cooling. So in His creating differences there is the establishment of evidence of perfection of power and execution of will, and this is a praiseworthy outcome. So the creation of all is wisdom. Likewise, when these different bodies assembled upon indicating that they have a wise maker who does not behave foolishly, a knowing one who is not ignorant, a powerful one who is not incapable, they are an evidence of perfection of power, completeness of management, and execution of will, since He made the opposites and differences, in respect of substance, homogeneous in the right of testimony to His lordship, His wisdom, and the perfection of His power. The third: that He created harmful bodies, pains, and ailments to humble by them the tyrants and kings, so that they may know by that their weakness, and not become insolent due to abundance of equipment and preparedness, and the gathering of armies and soldiers, followers and retinues. That becomes a motive for them to hasten to His obedience, a deterrent from plunging into His disobedience, an invitation to dispensing justice to those under their hands of subjects, and a prevention from injustice and oppression against those in their kingdoms from kinds of creatures. The fourth: that He created these harmful, disgusting things so that whoever sees them may know that He, exalted, is the originator of the world, independent, not benefiting from anything nor needing anything; for if that were not His description, His action would not go beyond what benefits and fulfills need. So in the creation of these things there is manifestation of His independence and His exaltation above need. Beyond these meanings that we have explained, there are many meanings that the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur - may God have mercy on him - mentioned in the chapters of al-Ta'dil wa al-Tajwir from the book al-Tawhid; we have refrained from mentioning them and sufficed with this amount, lest it lead to prolongation while the goal is achieved. The purpose with this amount, while we are not in need of explaining these things, for when we establish evidence of the oneness of the Maker, the impossibility of claiming two makers, that the world in all its parts is originated, its indication of its Originator, and that its Originator is wise, not foolish and not ignorant, the wisdom of creating these things is established necessarily from these premises. Then after that, the ignorance of the opponents of the aspect of wisdom remains, and that does not necessitate its departure from wisdom, as we have established. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (the second, third, and fourth) for the wisdom (ḥikma) in creation, following an initial point (not included here) about beneficial things validating divine promises and threats. The second argument states that God created the world with diverse parts, actions, and effects—both beneficial and harmful, good and bad, etc.—so that this diversity demonstrates the perfection of His power (qudra), the execution of His will (mashīʼa), and His sovereignty. The author notes that if a being acts in only one way, it is like a compelled force (e.g., fire always heats), but God's ability to produce opposites proves His absolute power and free will. The third argument states that God created harmful bodies, pains, and ailments to humble tyrants and kings, reminding them of their weakness so they do not become arrogant. This serves as a motive for obedience, a deterrent from sin, and an encouragement to justice. The fourth argument states that God created disgusting and harmful things to show that He is independent (ghanī) and has no needs, for a being that only creates beneficial things would appear dependent. Therefore, creating harmful things manifests His transcendence above need. The passage then concludes that these arguments are sufficient; the author refrains from listing all such purposes mentioned by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He emphasizes that once the core doctrines (oneness of God, creation, divine wisdom) are established, the wisdom of all creations follows necessarily. The opponents' ignorance of the specific wisdom does not negate its existence.

The Wisdom of Harmful Things: Demonstrating God's Independence
Nasafi argues that harmful things show God's independence and that wisdom is established necessarily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that creating harmful and disgusting things demonstrates God's independence from need, and that wisdom is established necessarily from fundamental doctrines.

Core Idea
Harmful creations show God's absolute independence, not His deficiency.

The fourth argument addresses a final purpose for harmful and disgusting creatures: they manifest God's independence (ghanī). If God only created beneficial things, one might think He needs those benefits. But by creating harmful things, God shows He is beyond benefit and need. The passage then concludes by emphasizing that once the foundational beliefs (tawḥīd, creation, divine wisdom) are proven, the wisdom of every creature follows necessarily—even if opponents fail to see it.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument carefully
Identify the key point: God creates harmful/disgusting things to show He is not dependent on benefit. This refutes the idea that a wise creator would only produce good.
2
Understand the concluding argument
The author asserts that once we establish God's oneness, that the world is created, and that the Creator is wise, then all creation is necessarily wise—even if we don't know the specific reason.
This is a powerful epistemological claim.
3
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how all four arguments together answer the objection that evil and harmful things contradict divine wisdom.

والنافع ليصح الوعد والإيعاد والترغيب والترهيب والله الموفق. والثاني: أنه خلق العالم مختلف الأجزاء متفاوت الأبعاض، متباين الأفعال والآثار من نافع وضار ومستحسن ومستقبح حار وبارد وخشن ولين وغير ذلك، ليستدل بذلك على كمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وسلطانه، إذ من فعله على جهة واحدة فهو كالمطبوع المسخر على ذلك كالنار على التسخين والثلج على التبريد، فيكون في خلقه المختلفات إقامة دلالة كمال القدرة ونفاذ المشيئة وهذه عاقبة حميدة، فكان خلق الكل حكمة وكذا لما اجتمعت هذه الأجسام المختلفة على الدلالة على أن لها صانعا حكيما لا يسفه، عالما لا يجهل قادرا لا يعجز كانت دليل كمال القدرة وتمام التدبير ونفاذ المشيئة، حيث جعل المتضادات والمختلفات بالجوهر متجانسا في حق الشهادة على ربوبيته وحكمته وكمال قدرته.
والثالث: أنه خلق الأجسام الضارة والأوجاع والآلام ليذل بها الجبابرة والملوك، ليعلموا بذلك ضعفهم، فلا يفتروا بكثرة العدة والعتاد، واجتماع الجنود والعساكر، والأتباع والحواشي، فيصير ذلك مبعثة لهم على المسارعة إلى طاعته، ومزجرة عن الانهماك في معاصيه وداعيا إلى إفاضة العدل فيمن تحت أيديهم من الرعايا، وصادًا لهم عن الجور والظلم على من في ممالكهم من أصناف البرايا.
والرابع: أنه خلق هذه الأشياء الضارة المستقذرة ليعرف من رآها أنه تعالى منشئ العالم غني أن ينتفع بشيء أو يحتاج إلى شيء إذ من ذلك وصفه لا يخرج فعله عما ينتفع به، وتندفع به حاجته فكان في تخليق هذه الأشياء إظهار غناه وتعاليه عن الحاجة، ووراء هذه المعاني التي بيناها معان كثيرة ذكرها الشيخ الإمام أبو منصور - رحمه الله - في مسائل التعديل والتجوير من كتاب التوحيد أعرضنا عن ذكرها، واكتفينا بهذا القدر لئلا يؤدي إلى التطويل مع حصول البغته والمقصود بهذا القدر مع أنا غير محتاجين إلى بيان هذه الأشياء فإنا إذا أقمنا الدلالة على وحدانية الصانع، وامتناع القول بالصانعين وعلى كون العالم بجميع أجزائه محدثاً ودلالته على منشئه، وعلى كون منشئه حكيما لا يسفه ولا يجهل، ثبت كون تخليق هذه الأشياء حكمة بضرورة هذه المقدمات، فبقي بعد ذلك جهل الخصوم بوجه الحكمة، وذا لا يوجب خروجه عن الحكمة على ما قررنا والله الوفق.
And the beneficial [creation is] to validate promise and threat, and encouragement and deterrence, and God is the grantor of success. The second: that He created the world with different parts, varying in limbs, diverse in actions and effects, from beneficial and harmful, good and bad, hot and cold, rough and soft, and other than that, so that by that it may be inferred the perfection of His power, the execution of His will, and His sovereignty; for whoever does a thing in one manner is like the imprinted subjugated to that, like fire for heating and snow for cooling. So in His creating differences there is the establishment of evidence of perfection of power and execution of will, and this is a praiseworthy outcome. So the creation of all is wisdom. Likewise, when these different bodies assembled upon indicating that they have a wise maker who does not behave foolishly, a knowing one who is not ignorant, a powerful one who is not incapable, they are an evidence of perfection of power, completeness of management, and execution of will, since He made the opposites and differences, in respect of substance, homogeneous in the right of testimony to His lordship, His wisdom, and the perfection of His power. Established Proofs Suffice to Show Wisdom
Nasafi concludes that proofs of God's oneness and wisdom vindicate creation despite evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Imam Nasafi, the existence of apparent evil does not contradict the wisdom of God's creation, because foundational theological proofs already establish that all creation is wise.

Core Idea
Proven truths about God already guarantee wisdom in creation.

Imam Nasafi brings his preceding discussion to a close by addressing a persistent doubt: how can evil or suffering exist if God is all-wise? He argues that we don't need to explain every specific instance of apparent harm, because the core proofs of God's oneness, the world's origination, and the Creator's perfect wisdom already make it certain that everything He creates is wise. This lesson examines that argument and shows why an opponent's ignorance of the specific wisdom does not undermine the truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read Nasafi's words carefully, then the literal translation. Notice how he builds his conclusion on earlier evidence.
This passage is a concluding argument, not an introduction.
2
Identify the core premise
Find the sentence that lists the established proofs (oneness, no two makers, world originated, Creator wise). Understand why these make the conclusion necessary.
The logic is: if these are proven, then wisdom in creation follows necessarily.
3
Understand the role of ignorance
Notice what Nasafi says about the opponents' lack of knowledge. He argues that their ignorance does not change the reality of wisdom.
This defends against the objection: 'We don't see the wisdom, so there must not be any.'
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why Nasafi mentions that he omitted many meanings from al-Maturidi's works. What is his teaching strategy here?
He prioritizes brevity and sufficiency: the core proofs are enough.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخرج فعله عما ينتفع به وتندفع به حاجته فكان في تخليق هذه الأشياء إظهار غناه وتعاليه عن الحاجة ووراء هذه المعاني التي بيناها معان كثيرة ذكرها الشيخ الإمام أبو منصور في مسائل التعديل والتجوير من كتاب التوحيد أعرضنا عن ذكرها واكتفينا بهذا القدر لئلا يؤدي إلى التطويل مع حصول البغته والمقصود بهذا القدر مع أنا غير محتاجين إلى بيان هذه الأشياء فإنا إذا أقمنا الدلالة على وحدانية الصانع وامتناع القول بالصانعين وعلى كون العالم بجميع أجزائه محدثاً ودلالته على منشئه وعلى كون منشئه حكيما لا يسفه ولا يجهل ثبت كون تخليق هذه الأشياء حكمة بضرورة هذه المقدمات فبقي بعد ذلك جهل الخصوم بوجه الحكمة وذا لا يوجب خروجه عن الحكمة على ما قررنا والله الوفق
His action goes beyond what benefits him and fulfills his need, so in the creation of these things was the manifestation of His self-sufficiency and His exaltedness above need. And beyond these meanings that we have explained, there are many meanings that the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur mentioned in the issues of justice and injustice from the book al-Tawhid, which we have omitted and sufficed with this amount to avoid prolongation while achieving the desired aim. And the intended purpose of this amount is that we are not in need of explaining these things, for when we establish the evidence for the uniqueness of the Maker, and the impossibility of saying two makers, and that the world in all its parts is originated, and its indication of its originator, and that its originator is wise, not foolish nor ignorant, then the creation of these things is established as wisdom by necessity from these premises. Then after that remains the ignorance of the opponents regarding the aspect of wisdom, and that does not necessitate its departure from wisdom, as we have established. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument in which Imam Nasafi shows that the existence of apparent harm or evil in creation does not contradict divine wisdom. He begins by stating that God's actions transcend human benefit and need, and creation manifests His self-sufficiency. He then notes that beyond the meanings already given, many more are mentioned in Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's works but are omitted here for brevity. The core argument is that the foundational proofs—of God's oneness, the impossibility of multiple creators, the world's origination, and the Creator's perfect wisdom—already establish that all creation is wise. The opponents' ignorance of the specific wisdom behind something does not negate that wisdom itself. Thus, the presence of evil or suffering does not undermine the truth of monotheism.

Misconception: Some think that the existence of evil or suffering disproves God's wisdom because we cannot see the benefit in each case.
Correction: Nasafi argues that once we have proven that God is one, the world is originated, and the Creator is wise, then all creation must be wise. Ignorance of the specific wisdom does not negate that wisdom.

Key Takeaway

Permissibility of Eating Animals to Preserve the Species
Nasafi argues that eating sheep is permissible because otherwise the species would perish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that eating animals (specifically sheep) is permissible because it preserves the species.

Core Idea
Eating animals preserves their species; prohibition would destroy them.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents a practical argument for the permissibility of eating sheep. He observes that sheep cannot survive without human care, so allowing humans to benefit from them ensures their preservation. This argument uses the preservation of the species as a justification for the permission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the vivid description of sheep's helplessness.
2
Understand the condition of sheep
Nasafi describes sheep as defenseless: no speed, no wings, no natural weapons. They also panic when alone. Identify these traits in the text.
The description builds a case for their dependence on humans.
3
Follow the logical chain
The argument: if humans cannot benefit from sheep, they won't care for them → sheep perish → species extinct. Therefore, permission is necessary for preservation. Trace this chain in the passage.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi ends with a strong condemnation: whoever forbids eating them is ignorant and foolish. Consider why he uses such strong language.
The conclusion ties the argument to a moral judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: إن الجنس الذي جعل غذاء للبشر من جملة الحيوانات هو الغنم، ومن المعلوم الذي لا ريب فيه أن هذا الجنس لو خلا عن قيام الآدمي بدفع أسباب الهلاك عنه، وإيصال ما فيه منفعته ومصلحته إليه، ولا امتناع له لا بالقوائم ولا بالجناح، وليس معه آلة الدفاع والحراب فتمزقه السباع من أوهى مدة أسرع وقت، وكذا من طبع هذا الجنس أن راعيها لو بعد عنها في المفاوز والصحاري لبقيت رافعة رؤوسها إلى السماء متخوفة عن كل صوت، نافرة عن كل ذكر لا تتناول علفا، ولا ترد ماء إلى أن تتلف جوعا وعطشا. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فلو لم يبح للآدمي الانتفاع بها وتناول لحومها، لما قام بالدفع عنها والحفظ لها فيتلف الكل وينقطع الجنس، فكان إباحة التناول إبقاء للجنس، وفي الحظر إهلاكه، فمن لم يبح إتلاف البعض ليصير ذلك طريقا إلى فناء الكل فلا علاج لجهله وحمقه.
Then it is said to them: The genus that is made as food for humans among animals is sheep. And it is known without doubt that this genus, if it were devoid of humans undertaking to repel causes of destruction from it and to deliver what benefits it to it—and it has no resistance by legs or wings, nor any tool of defense or spear—then beasts would tear it apart in the weakest period and quickest time. Likewise, it is of the nature of this genus that if its shepherd were to distance himself from it in the deserts and wildernesses, it would remain lifting its heads to the sky, fearful of every sound, fleeing from every male, not taking fodder nor returning to water until it perishes from hunger and thirst. And when the matter is so, if the human were not permitted to benefit from it and eat its meat, he would not undertake to defend and preserve it, so the whole would perish and the genus would become extinct. Thus, permitting consumption preserves the genus, and forbidding it destroys it. So whoever does not permit the destruction of part in order to avoid the annihilation of the whole—there is no remedy for his ignorance and foolishness.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a teleological argument for the permissibility of eating animals, specifically sheep. The author observes that sheep are unable to defend themselves or survive without human care; they lack legs for running, wings for flight, and natural weapons. Furthermore, if left alone, they become paralyzed with fear and starve. Therefore, human intervention is necessary for their survival. If humans are allowed to benefit from them (by eating their meat), they will maintain them; otherwise, the species would go extinct. The author concludes that forbidding the killing of some animals would lead to the destruction of the entire genus, so permission is a preservation of the species, and prohibition would be foolish.

Key Terms
الجنس
الغنم
إباحة
الإبقاء
الإهلاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this justifies cruelty to animals or unnecessary killing.
Correction: The argument is about species preservation and the role of humans as stewards. It does not endorse cruelty but uses the fact that sheep depend on humans for survival, and permission to use them ensures their care and continuation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if humans were not permitted to eat sheep?
A) The sheep would become wild and dangerous.
B) The sheep would all die of old age.
C) The sheep would not be cared for and the species would perish.
D) The sheep would learn to defend themselves.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of eating sheep is grounded in the preservation of the species, as human care is necessary for their survival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author calls those who would forbid consumption 'ignorant and foolish.' Are there any beliefs or practices you hold that might unintentionally lead to harm or extinction, even if well-intentioned? What would it take for you to reconsider them?
?
If you truly believe that humans are stewards of creation, how does this argument challenge you to actively participate in the preservation of animals beyond just eating them? What practical steps can you take this week?

Why Divine Attributes Are Not Accidents
The author redefines accident and refutes the attribution of accidents to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine attributes cannot be classified as accidents and how the Maturidi position refutes both the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya sects.

Core Idea
An accident is defined by impossibility of persistence, not by lacking self-subsistence — so God cannot be an accident.

The debate over divine attributes often led to false analogies with created accidents. In this passage, the author clarifies the true definition of 'accident' and systematically proves that God transcends this category entirely, avoiding the errors of both Mu'tazila and Karramiyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the author's rhetorical structure: he first rejects both sects, then redefines accident, then builds a logical proof.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the redefinition of 'accident'
The author argues that an accident is called such because of the impossibility of its persistence, not because it cannot subsist by itself. This is crucial because it decouples the concept from self-subsistence.
3
Follow the logical refutation
The author then shows that impossibility of persistence cannot apply to God (since He is pre-eternal), and the concomitant of an accident—dependence on a locus—is impossible for the Creator. Trace each premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلا المذهبين فاسد، فإن العرض في الشاهد كان عرضا لاستحالة بقائه، لأنه هو المعنى الذي ينبئ عن المعنى اللغوي، لا لاستحالة قيامه بذاته إذ لا دلالة في اللغة تدل على أن ما لا قيام له بذاته، يسمى عرضا ، فإذا ثبت أن الأمر على ما بينا، لم تكن صفات الله تعالى أعراضا ، ولم يلزمنا بإثبات الصفات الله تعالى إثبات الأعراض في الأزل، ولا القول بقيام الأعراض بذات الله تعالى. وإذا تقرر هذا، ثم تأملنا فعلمنا أن المعنى الذي لأجله كان العرض عرضا، وهو استحالة البقاء مستحيل على الباري عز وعلا، إذ القول بقدم ما لا بقاء له محال، بل هو كما يوجد ينعدم في الثاني من حال وجوده، وعلمنا أن ما هو من لوازم العرض وهو استحالة قيامه بذاته، وافتقاره إلى محل يقوم به محال ثبوته فيمن تولى إنشاء العالم، إذ قيام الفعل بما لا قيام له بذاته محال وكذا الفعل المحكم المتقن لا يتأتى إلا من قادر حي عالم، واتصاف ما لا قيام له بذاته عالما قادرا حيا محال وكذا افتقار القديم إلى المحل محال، إذ شرط القدم الاستغناء في الوجود عن غيره، وكذا القول بقدم المكان محال، لقيام دليل حدوث ما سوى الله تعالى ووجود العرض بلا محل يقوم به محال، فإذا علمنا بالوقوف على هذه المقدمات الصادقة أن الباري جل وعلا ليس بعرض ويستحيل وصفه به، والله الموفق.
And both doctrines are corrupt. For the accident in the witnessed world was an accident because of the impossibility of its persistence, because that is the meaning that indicates the linguistic meaning, not because of the impossibility of its subsistence by itself, since there is no linguistic indication that what does not subsist by itself is called an accident. So when it is established that the matter is as we have explained, then the attributes of God the Exalted are not accidents, and we are not compelled by affirming the attributes of God the Exalted to affirm the existence of accidents in pre-eternity, nor to affirm the subsistence of accidents in God's essence. And when this is settled, then we reflected and we knew that the meaning for which the accident is an accident—namely the impossibility of persistence—is impossible for the Creator, Mighty and Majestic, for the statement of the pre-eternality of that which has no persistence is impossible; rather, it exists and then ceases to exist in the second moment of its existence. And we knew that what is a necessary concomitant of the accident—namely the impossibility of its subsistence by itself, and its need for a locus in which to subsist—its affirmation is impossible for the One who undertakes the creation of the world, for the subsistence of a action in that which does not subsist by itself is impossible, and likewise, a firm and precise action can only proceed from a powerful, living, knowing being, and the description of that which does not subsist by itself as knowing, powerful, living is impossible. Likewise, the need of the pre-eternal for a locus is impossible, for the condition of pre-eternality is independence from others in existence. Likewise, the statement of the pre-eternality of place is impossible, due to the proof of the temporal origination of everything other than God, and the existence of an accident without a locus in which to subsist is impossible. So when we know, by pausing at these true premises, that the Creator, Glorious and Exalted, is not an accident and it is impossible to describe Him as such. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage refutes both the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya positions. The author first reasserts his earlier definition: an accident is an accident because of the impossibility of its persistence, not because it lacks self-subsistence. Therefore, divine attributes are not accidents, and affirming them does not force one to accept accidents in pre-eternity or accidents subsisting in God. Then the author proceeds to prove that God cannot be an accident at all. The reason for being an accident (impossibility of persistence) cannot apply to God, because that would entail temporal origination. The necessary concomitants of an accident—dependence on a locus and inability to subsist by itself—also cannot belong to God, because the Creator must be self-sufficient, and His actions (creation) require a powerful, living, knowing agent. Moreover, the pre-eternal cannot depend on a locus, and place cannot be pre-eternal. Thus, from these true premises, it is established that God is not an accident and cannot be described as such.

استحالة بقائه
قيام بذاته
قدم
محل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation of divine attributes being accidents relies only on the definition of 'arad.
Correction: The author provides multiple rational arguments beyond the definition, including impossibility of persistence for the pre-eternal and the impossibility of the Creator depending on a locus.

D) They are not found in the witnessed world

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are not accidents because the defining characteristic of an accident—impossibility of persistence—is impossible for the pre-eternal and self-sufficient Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author insists that the defining feature of an accident is its inability to persist. Consider the things you rely on daily—your health, wealth, relationships, even your own abilities. Are they not like accidents, here today and gone tomorrow? How does this truth confront your tendency to anchor your heart in transient things, rather than in the eternal attributes of God? What would it look like for you to transfer your trust from the passing to the Permanent, starting today?

If God's attributes—His power, knowledge, life—are not accidents, they are not subject to change or cessation. When was the last time you acted as if something about God might change? Maybe you doubted His forgiveness after a repeated sin, or feared His plan would fail. How does this lesson's logic expose the folly of such doubts? Will you commit to reminding yourself daily that His attributes are as eternal as He is?
?
The passage concludes that God cannot depend on a locus or need anything outside Himself. You, by contrast, depend on countless things for your very existence. How does this radical independence of God challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? Are there areas of your life where you still believe you can manage without Him, relying on your own 'locus' of strength? Refuting That Allah's Attributes Are Accidents
Refutes claim that Allah's attributes are accidents and proves He is not an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attributes of Allah cannot be considered accidents, based on the correct definition of accident and the implications of pre-eternity.

Core Idea
An accident is defined by impermanence, not by subsisting in another.

The claim that Allah's attributes are accidents arises from a misunderstanding of the term 'accident' (ʿaraḍ). In this passage, the author clarifies the real definition of accident and shows that applying it to Allah leads to absurdities. You will see how careful linguistic and theological analysis safeguards divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author begins by stating the opponents' claim and then immediately refutes it.
2
Analyze the definition of accident
Focus on where the author defines 'accident' as that which cannot persist, not as that which subsists in another. This is crucial for the entire argument.
3
Follow the proof that Allah is not an accident
Trace the argument that the impossibility of remaining and the need for a substrate are impossible for the Creator. This leads to the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنها لا تقوم بذواتها، بل تقوم بذاته تعالى سمتها أعراضا لوجود ما كان العرض لأجله عرضا في الشاهد.
[إبطال ادعائهم]:
وكلا المذهبين فاسد، فإن العرض في الشاهد كان عرضا لاستحالة بقائه، لأنه هو المعنى الذي ينبئ عن المعنى اللغوي، لا لاستحالة قيامه بذاته إذ لا دلالة في اللغة تدل على أن ما لا قيام له بذاته، يسمى عرضا ، فإذا ثبت أن الأمر على ما بينا، لم تكن صفات الله تعالى أعراضا ، ولم يلزمنا بإثبات الصفات الله تعالى إثبات الأعراض في الأزل، ولا القول بقيام الأعراض بذات الله تعالى. وإذا تقرر هذا، ثم تأملنا فعلمنا أن المعنى الذي لأجله كان العرض عرضا، وهو استحالة البقاء مستحيل على الباري عز وعلا، إذ القول بقدم ما لا بقاء له محال، بل هو كما يوجد ينعدم في الثاني من حال وجوده، وعلمنا أن ما هو من لوازم العرض وهو استحالة قيامه بذاته، وافتقاره إلى محل يقوم به محال ثبوته فيمن تولى إنشاء العالم، إذ قيام الفعل بما لا قيام له بذاته محال ( وكذا الفعل المحكم المتقن لا يتأتى إلا من قادر حي عالم ، واتصاف ما لا قيام له بذاته عالما قادرا حيا محال) وكذا افتقار القديم إلى المحل محال ، إذ شرط القدم الاستغناء في الوجود عن غيره، وكذا القول بقدم المكان محال ، لقيام دليل حدوث ما سوى الله تعالى ووجود العرض بلا محل يقوم به محال، فإذا علمنا بالوقوف على هذه المقدمات الصادقة أن الباري جل وعلا ليس بعرض ويستحيل وصفه به، والله الموفق.
They do not subsist by themselves, but they subsist by His essence, so they are called 'accidents' due to the presence of that for which an accident is an accident in the witnessed world. [Invalidation of their claim]: Both schools of thought are corrupt, for the accident in the witnessed world was an accident due to the impossibility of its remaining, because it is the meaning that indicates the linguistic meaning, not due to the impossibility of its subsisting by itself, since there is no linguistic indication that whatever does not subsist by itself is called an accident. So when it is established that the matter is as we have clarified, the attributes of Allah, exalted, are not accidents, and we are not compelled by affirming the attributes of Allah to affirm accidents in pre-eternity, nor to affirm that accidents subsist in the essence of Allah. And when this is settled, then we reflect and know that the meaning for which an accident is an accident—which is the impossibility of remaining—is impossible for the Creator, mighty and sublime, for claiming pre-eternity for that which has no remaining is impossible; rather, it is as it exists, it ceases in the second moment of its existence. And we know that what is among the necessary concomitants of an accident—namely, the impossibility of its subsisting by itself and its need for a substrate in which it subsists—is impossible to affirm for the One who undertakes the creation of the world, for the subsistence of an act in that which does not subsist by itself is impossible (and likewise, the precise, perfect act cannot be produced except from a powerful, living, knowing [agent], and the description of that which does not subsist by itself as knowing, powerful, living is impossible). And likewise, the need of the pre-eternal for a substrate is impossible, for the condition of pre-eternity is self-sufficiency in existence from others. And likewise, the claim of pre-eternity of place is impossible, because of the proof for the temporal origination of everything other than Allah, and the existence of an accident without a substrate in which it subsists is impossible. So when we know, by grasping these true premises, that the Creator, glorious and exalted, is not an accident and that describing Him as such is impossible, and Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that Allah's attributes are accidents and establishes that Allah Himself is not an accident. The author begins by noting the definition of 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) in the witnessed world: it is that which does not subsist by itself but subsists in a substrate, and it is called an accident due to the impossibility of its remaining, not due to its dependence on a substrate. The opponents claim that because Allah's attributes subsist in His essence, they are accidents, but the author shows this is false: the linguistic term 'accident' is based on impermanence, not on not subsisting by itself. Thus, affirming attributes for Allah does not entail affirming accidents in pre-eternity or accidents subsisting in His essence. Next, the author argues that the defining characteristic of an accident—impossibility of remaining—is impossible for Allah, since that would require a temporal entity to be pre-eternal, which is absurd. Moreover, the necessary concomitants of an accident, such as needing a substrate and not subsisting by itself, cannot be attributed to the Creator of the world, because a perfect act requires a powerful, living, knowing agent, and one who does not subsist by itself cannot possess these attributes. The need of the pre-eternal for a substrate is impossible because pre-eternity entails self-sufficiency. Finally, the author concludes that from these premises it is known that Allah is not an accident and cannot be described as such.

Correction: An accident is defined by impermanence, not by subsisting in another. Since Allah's attributes are eternal, they are not accidents.

Key Takeaway
